L2_LKUP could return "unknown" and trigger a broadcast that became a multicast storm or a drop. The HAIRPIN stage solves this with a flow at a higher priority than L2_LKUP that sets `reg15 = inport_idx` (output back to the original inport, which OVN allows through an `inport != outport` check) and runs `next` to skip L2_LKUP.

**Problems it solves.** Specifically: (a) output the packet back to the backend, which is also the client on the same LSP, (b) skip L2_LKUP to avoid FDB miss and broadcast, (c) allow output to the same inport (OVS by default rejects `inport == outport`, so a bypass flag is needed). The solution: a flow `match=(reg0[6] == 1)` at high priority with the action `eth.dst = <backend_mac>; reg15 = <backend_port_idx>; output;`, with a bypass flag for the inport check via `MLF_FORCE_LOCAL` or equivalent. Scope: stateful self-LB packets only.

**Importance.** Medium. Required for the self-LB scenario. Without this stage, packets drop at L2_LKUP or loop through broadcast. The engineer should remember that HAIRPIN is the final stage of a three-stage chain, not PRE_HAIRPIN or NAT_HAIRPIN.

**How it works.** `northd/northd.c` `build_lb_hairpin` (the same function as for NAT_HAIRPIN) emits the HAIRPIN stage flows:

```
table=15 (ls_in_hairpin), priority=100,
  match=(ip && reg0[6] == 1),
  action=(eth.dst = <backend_mac>;
          outport = <backend_lsp_uuid>;
          flags.loopback = 1;   /* bypass inport==outport check */
          output;)

table=15 (ls_in_hairpin), priority=0,
  match=(1), action=(next;)
```

`flags.loopback = 1` sets MLF_ALLOW_LOOPBACK to allow output to the inport (OVS rejects this by default to prevent loops). The backend MAC and LSP UUID are resolved from the VIP backend list at compile time.

**How an operator masters this.** (1) **Read**: understand that HAIRPIN bypasses L2_LKUP to avoid broadcast. (2) **Modify**: no direct configuration. The stage activates automatically when a hairpin LB is set up. (3) **Observe**: run `ovn-trace` on a self-LB packet through to stage 14 and verify that the outport is set to the correct backend LSP. (4) **Debug**: if the packet drops at this stage, check the `flags.loopback` action or the `MLF_ALLOW_LOOPBACK` flag.

**Classification.** Stage type: **output shortcut** (subtype "self-LB delivery"). Different from `LS_IN_L2_LKUP` (generic L2 unicast) and from `LS_IN_L2_UNKNOWN` (BUM flooding). Same family as the ARP responder stages (`LS_IN_ARP_ND_RSP`) in that it short-circuits the main forwarding logic.

**Usage workflow.** The stage activates automatically when the PRE_HAIRPIN and NAT_HAIRPIN flows exist. Verification procedure:
1. Set up self-LB (see ¬ß13.19.13).
2. Verify the HAIRPIN flow: `ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls-app | grep ls_in_hairpin` should show a flow at priority 100.
3. Test a self-LB packet: from a VM that is also a backend, call the VIP.
4. Run `ovn-trace` and verify that at stage 14 the outport equals the backend LSP UUID with `flags.loopback=1`.
5. Verify that the packet reaches the backend: `tcpdump -ni vnet0` on the VM should show the incoming packet with `src = hairpin_snat_ip`.
6. Verify the reply path: the backend reply traverses the reverse hairpin chain (un-SNAT, un-DNAT) and reaches the client.

**When something goes wrong.** Symptom: "the self-LB packet does not reach the backend; it drops at stage 14". Diagnostic step-by-step:
- Step 1: run `ovn-trace` on the packet and check whether the HAIRPIN stage matches priority 100.
- Step 2: if the match works but the outport is not set, this may be a compile bug (failed backend resolution). Restart `ovn-northd`.
- Step 3: if the outport is set correctly but OVS drops with reason "inport==outport", the `flags.loopback` action is not set. Check the `ovn-controller` version (versions older than 22.03 do not support it).
- Step 4: if the packet reaches the backend but the MAC does not match, the backend interface filters it. Check that `eth.dst` is the correct backend MAC.

Cross-link Part 13.19 line 657 (MLF_RCV_FROM_RAMP), Part 13.18 ¬ßMLF_ALLOW_LOOPBACK.

**Tightly related to.** Tightly coupled: `LS_IN_PRE_HAIRPIN` (upstream chain), `LS_IN_NAT_HAIRPIN` (upstream SNAT), the `MLF_ALLOW_LOOPBACK` flag, the `flags.loopback` action, `reg0[6]` REGBIT_HAIRPIN, and the backend LSP UUID and MAC. Loose: `LS_IN_L2_LKUP` (the bypassed stage) and `LS_IN_L2_UNKNOWN` (the broadcast stage that is avoided).

**Version differences.**
- OVN 22.03: introduces the HAIRPIN stage with `flags.loopback`.
- OVN 22.06 and 22.09: stable.
- OVN 23.06 and later: the stage shifts to 17.
- OVN 24.03: integrates `MLF_RCV_FROM_RAMP` to prevent a hairpin loop from the ramp-switch path (see line 657).

**Observation and verification.**

```
$ ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls1 | grep -A3 "ls_in_hairpin"
  table=15 (ls_in_hairpin), priority=100,
    match=(ip && reg0[6] == 1),
    action=(eth.dst = 50:54:00:00:00:20;
            outport = "lsp-app1"; flags.loopback = 1; output;)

$ ovn-trace --minimal ls-app 'inport=="lsp-app1" && eth.src==... && ...
                              && ip4.src==10.0.0.20 && ip4.dst==10.0.0.100
                              && tcp.dst==80'
  ingress(dp=ls-app, inport=lsp-app1)
   ...
   13. ls_in_pre_hairpin: priority 100, reg0[6] = 1
   14. ls_in_nat_hairpin: priority 100, ct_snat_to_vip
   15. ls_in_hairpin: reg0[6] == 1, priority 100
       eth.dst = 50:54:00:00:00:20
       outport = "lsp-app1"
       flags.loopback = 1
       output;
```

**Source code reference.**
- `northd/northd.c` `build_lb_hairpin()`: the same function as for NAT_HAIRPIN, generating all three hairpin stages.
- `controller/lflow.c` handles `flags.loopback = 1` and sets MLF_ALLOW_LOOPBACK.
- `northd/northd.h` `S_SWITCH_IN_HAIRPIN = 14` (branch-22.03).
- Pre-existing reference: Part 13.16 line 166 and line 62 (CT_SNAT_HAIRPIN OFTABLE).

**Real-world incident.** Q4 2024, an OpenStack production cluster. The customer reported that "self-LB works in unit tests but fails in the production cluster after port mirroring is enabled". Investigation: `ovn-trace` showed the HAIRPIN stage matching priority 100 correctly, with the outport set to the right backend LSP, but OVS dropped the packet with reason "loopback prevented". Root cause: the port mirror feature used a SPAN destination port that overlapped with the backend LSP, and OVS rejected the loopback even with `flags.loopback=1`. Fix: move the port-mirror destination to a separate port (not overlapping with any backend), then verify that hairpin works again. Cross-link Part 9.7 ¬ßport mirroring side-effects.

**Synthetic exercise.**

```bash
# Prerequisite: the labs in ¬ß13.19.13 and ¬ß13.19.14 have been set up.
# Lab task 1: run ovn-trace on the self-LB packet and verify that stage 14 (HAIRPIN) sets the outport to the correct backend LSP.
sudo ovn-trace --minimal ls-hairpin \
  'inport == "lsp-app1" && eth.src == 50:54:00:00:00:20
   && eth.dst == 50:54:00:00:00:01 && ip4.src == 10.0.0.20
   && ip4.dst == 10.0.0.100 && tcp.dst == 80'
# Lab task 2: tcpdump on app1 should show the incoming packet with eth.dst = MAC of app1.
# Lab task 3: send 100 self-LB requests and count the successes (expect 100 percent).
# Lab task 4: try outputting to an LSP that is not the backend (expect a drop, negative test).
# Lab task 5: verify ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int | grep -E "table=15.*loopback" shows MLF_ALLOW_LOOPBACK set.
```

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.**
- Mode A "drop with reason loopback": the `flags.loopback` action is not set. Check that the OVN version is 22.03 or later and that `ovn-controller` matches.
- Mode B "outport set incorrectly": the backend LSP UUID did not resolve. Restart `ovn-northd`.
- Mode C "incorrect dst MAC": backend MAC mismatch (the LSP has multiple MACs). Check that `Logical_Switch_Port.addresses` is correct.
- Mode D "stage bypass but L2_LKUP still runs": the HAIRPIN flow priority is lower than the L2_LKUP fallback (a compile-structure error). Check priority 100 versus the L2_LKUP priority.

**Comparison with other systems.** A Linux bridge with `hairpin_mode=on` on a port is the closest analog: it enables loopback on one port. NSX-T LB hairpin output through direct mapping is equivalent. AWS NLB handles hairpin internally. Cisco ACE one-arm mode solves the same problem with VLAN tags or a routing trick. The OVN approach (declarative compile-time flows plus `flags.loopback`) is cleaner than the iptables hairpin patchwork.

---

## ¬ß13.19.16 LR_IN_DEFRAG, IP defragmentation for LB and NAT lookup

**Concept.** `LR_IN_DEFRAG` is the Logical_Router ingress logical stage that **reassembles IP fragments** (IPv4 fragments or IPv6 Fragment headers) into a complete packet before the pipeline reaches Load_Balancer DNAT (`LR_IN_DNAT`) or pure SNAT/DNAT rules (`LR_IN_UNSNAT`, `LR_IN_DNAT`). The reason: both LB and NAT need to read the TCP or UDP port to hash or match the tuple, and the TCP or UDP port lives only in the first fragment. Without reassembly, fragments 2 to N are skipped by NAT and the connection breaks. Type: **stateful preparation stage** at the medium tier, subtype "L3 reassembly".

**History and background.** The stage was introduced by Han Zhou and Numan Siddique together with the OVN distributed gateway port in 21.03 (2021), when the first north-south LB had to handle IPv4 fragmented traffic from the Internet (browser file uploads, MTU mismatch). In OVN 20.06 to 20.12, only `LR_IN_DNAT` existed and there was no reassembly. This led to a production issue, "LB drops packets larger than the MTU", in environments with broken Path-MTU-Discovery. OVN 22.03 stabilised the stage, sharing the `ct_next` action with LS_IN_PRE_ACL but using a separate OF zone (the routing zone instead of the switch zone).

**Position in the architecture.** Logical stage 5 maps to OF table 13 on br-int (`8 + 5 = 13`, per the formula in Part 13.16 line 220). It follows `LR_IN_UNSNAT` (logical 4) and precedes `LR_IN_DNAT` (logical 6). The critical ordering is noted in Part 13.16 line 240: UNSNAT comes before DEFRAG because reply packets reaching the router with the SNAT-translated destination must be un-SNAT-ed first. DEFRAG sits in the middle because DNAT needs to access the L4 header (the port number), so the packet must be defragmented. Scope: per-packet, per-LR datapath.

**Role.** The main role: **ensure the packet has a complete L4 header** (TCP or UDP source and destination ports) before the LB or DNAT stages. The secondary role: trigger the routing conntrack zone (`zone=<lr_zone>`) via the `ct_next` action so that every fragment is reassembled by the kernel netfilter reassembly module. Scope: only IP packets with the `ip.frag` flag set (MF or last fragment). Normal packets fall through to priority 0 `next`.

**Why it exists.** Before OVN 21.03, IP fragments arriving at an LR went straight into the DNAT pipeline. The OVN DNAT action compiles into `ct(commit, nat(dst=...))`, but the OVS kernel datapath conntrack only commits the first fragment correctly (the one with L4 headers). Fragments 2 to N (L3 only) do not match, do not get the `nat()` rewrite, and arrive at the backend with the destination IP still set to the original VIP (unrewritten by DNAT). They are then dropped. Production pain point: file uploads through the LB failed intermittently for users with MTU 1280 (mobile or GRE tunnel). The DEFRAG stage was added to call `ct_next` solely for reassembly, with the kernel netfilter module (`nf_defrag_ipv4.ko`) handling the actual reassembly.

**Problems it solves.** Specifically: (a) IPv4 or IPv6 fragments 2 to N have no L4 header, (b) LB hash on 5-tuple needs the port number, (c) NAT rules need to match the destination port. Solution: a priority 100 flow that matches `ip.is_frag` and runs `ct_next` (entering the routing conntrack zone for kernel reassembly), and a priority 0 flow with `next` for non-fragment packets. After reassembly, the packet returns to the pipeline at the next stage with the full L3 and L4 headers. Scope: only fragmentation at the IP layer. Fragmentation at the TCP segment level (segmentation offload) is out of scope and is handled by the TCP stacks at either end.

**Importance.** Medium. Required for every LR that has an LB or NAT rule and processes traffic that may be fragmented. If the stage is disabled (compile bug or custom build), the customer sees intermittent failures that are very hard to debug: connections establish correctly but large file downloads fail. Operator essential: this stage is the one to remember when the symptom is "the LB works for small requests but fails for large ones".

**How it works.** The flow generator function is `build_lrouter_in_defrag_flow` in `northd/northd.c` (branch-22.03). The flow template:

```
table=13 (lr_in_defrag), priority=100,
  match=(ip),
  action=(reg0 = ip4.dst; reg1 = ip4.src; ct_next;)

table=13 (lr_in_defrag), priority=0,
  match=(1), action=(next;)
```

The `ct_next` action in `lib/actions.c` parses into `ofpact_conntrack` with the flag `OFPACT_CT_DEFRAG_ONLY`. The controller translates it to OVS `ct(table=<next>, zone=NX_REG11[0..15])` with the dedicated conntrack zone `OFTABLE_CT_DEFRAG_ZONE`. The kernel module `nf_defrag_ipv4.ko` (Linux 5.x) performs the actual reassembly inside `nf_ct_frag6_gather()` (IPv6) or `nf_ct_frag4_gather()` (older IPv4) or the integrated `ip_defrag()`. Invariant: after this stage, every packet that reaches the next stage has a complete L4 header (or it has been dropped due to reassembly timeout).

**How an operator masters this.** Four skills: (1) **Read**: recognise that a `ct_next` flow in table 13 on br-int means DEFRAG is active (`ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int table=13 | grep ct`). (2) **Modify**: no direct configuration. The stage activates automatically when the LR has an LB or NAT rule. Tune `nf_conntrack_frag6_timeout` (default 60s for IPv6) or `ipfrag_time` (default 30s for IPv4) via sysctl. (3) **Observe**: `ovs-appctl dpctl/dump-conntrack zone=<lr_zone>` shows entries with state `EXPECTED` for fragments currently being reassembled. (4) **Debug**: if you suspect a fragment drop, use `ofproto/trace 'in_port=N,ip,ip_frag=yes'` to verify whether the DEFRAG flow is hit.

**Classification.** Stage type: **stateful preparation** (subtype "L3 reassembly"). Different from `LS_IN_PRE_ACL` (defrag for the stateful ACL side, switch zone) and from `LR_IN_DNAT` (DNAT commit, which comes later). Same family as `LS_IN_PRE_LB` and `LS_IN_PRE_STATEFUL` in that they "prepare state for the next stateful stage".

**Usage workflow.** The stage activates automatically when an LB or NAT rule is created on the LR. Verification procedure:
1. Set up the LR and LB: `ovn-nbctl lr-add lr1 && ovn-nbctl lb-add lb1 10.0.0.100:80 10.0.0.10:80,10.0.0.11:80 && ovn-nbctl lr-lb-add lr1 lb1`.
2. Verify the DEFRAG flows: `ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr1 | grep "lr_in_defrag"` should show two flows at priorities 100 and 0.
3. Verify the OF flow on the hypervisor: `ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int table=13 | grep ct_next`.
4. Test with a fragment: use `scapy` to send an IP packet with `ip_frag=1, MF=1` and check that `dpctl/dump-conntrack` has an entry in the routing zone.
5. Verify that the reassembled packet reaches DNAT correctly: `ovn-trace lr1 'inport=="lrp1" && ip4.src==1.2.3.4 && ip4.dst==10.0.0.100 && ip.frag==1'`.

**When something goes wrong.** Typical symptoms: (a) "the LB works for small HTTP requests but fails for large file uploads", which suggests a DEFRAG miss, a kernel reassembly timeout, or the `nf_defrag` module is not loaded. (b) "the connection establishes correctly but resets after a few seconds", which suggests reassembly timeout (default 30s for IPv4) while fragments 2 to N are delayed, so a fragment is lost. Diagnostic flow:
- Step 1: `cat /proc/sys/net/ipv4/ipfrag_time` to read the timeout.
- Step 2: `cat /proc/net/netfilter/nf_conntrack_expect` to view pending expected entries.
- Step 3: `lsmod | grep nf_defrag` to check whether the module is loaded.
- Step 4: `tcpdump -ni eth0 'ip[6:2] & 0x1fff != 0 or ip[6] & 0x20 != 0'` to capture fragments and verify the rate.

Cross-link Part 19.0 ¬ßPMTUD + reassembly, Part 13.3 ¬ßLB DNAT.

**Tightly related to.** Tightly coupled: `LR_IN_UNSNAT` (upstream, logical 4), `LR_IN_DNAT` (downstream, logical 6, the consumer of the reassembled packet), `nf_defrag_ipv4.ko` and `nf_defrag_ipv6.ko` (kernel modules that perform the actual work), the routing conntrack zone `lr_zone` (OFTABLE_CT_DEFRAG_ZONE), and the `ip.is_frag` predicate in the action grammar. Loose: `LS_IN_PRE_ACL` (same `ct_next` pattern but with the switch zone).

**Version differences.**
- OVN 21.03 (2021): introduces the stage for distributed gateway north-south LB.
- OVN 21.06: adds support for the IPv6 Fragment header (Next Header 44).
- OVN 22.03 (curriculum baseline): the routing and switch zones are fully separated.
- OVN 23.06: adds a separate stage `LR_IN_DEFRAG_ECMP_STATEFUL` for ECMP stateful, while the generic DEFRAG remains unchanged.
- OVN 24.03: optimisation, folds `ct_next` into `ct(table=..., nat)` when there is no LB, saving one conntrack lookup.

**C√°ch quan s√°t + x√°c minh.**

```
$ ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr1 | grep -A2 "lr_in_defrag"
  table=5 (lr_in_defrag), priority=100,
    match=(ip), action=(reg0 = ip4.dst; reg1 = ip4.src; ct_next;)
  table=5 (lr_in_defrag), priority=0,
    match=(1), action=(next;)

$ ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int table=13 | head -3
  cookie=0x1, table=13, n_packets=42, ip, actions=ct(table=14,zone=NXM_NX_REG11[0..15])

$ ovs-appctl dpctl/dump-conntrack zone=8000 | head -3
  tcp,orig=(src=1.2.3.4,dst=10.0.0.100,sport=33000,dport=80),
       reply=(src=10.0.0.10,dst=1.2.3.4,sport=80,dport=33000),
       protoinfo=(state=ESTABLISHED), zone=8000
```

**Source code reference.**
- `northd/northd.c` `build_lrouter_in_defrag_flow()`: the function that emits flows for this stage (verified at branch-22.03).
- `lib/actions.c` parser for the `ct_next` action with the DEFRAG_ONLY flag.
- `controller/lflow.c` translates `ct_next` to OVS `ct(table=..., zone=...)`.
- `northd/ovn-northd.8.xml` ¬ß"Ingress Table 5: DEFRAG" documentation text.
- Kernel: `net/netfilter/nf_conntrack_proto.c` `nf_conntrack_handle_packet` integration with defrag.
- Pre-existing reference: Part 13.16 line 220 and line 240 (the UNSNAT, DEFRAG, DNAT ordering).

**Real-world incident.** Q3 2024, an OpenStack production cluster with OVN 22.03. The customer used a north-south LB to expose service 10.10.0.100:443 for a mobile app. Reports indicated 5 to 15 percent of file uploads larger than 1.4 MB failed depending on the network. Investigation: tcpdump on the external uplink showed IPv4 fragments (because PMTUD was blocked by a firewall along the path), and `ovs-appctl dpctl/dump-conntrack zone=<lr_zone>` showed entries in `EXPECTED` state that never progressed to ESTABLISHED, indicating a reassembly timeout. Root cause: the hypervisor host's default `ipfrag_time` was 30s, but the mobile network had high RTT and jitter, and fragment 2 arrived more than 30s late. Fix: `sysctl -w net.ipv4.ipfrag_time=60` and `net.ipv4.ipfrag_high_thresh=8388608` (8 MB) applied to every compute node. The drop rate fell below 0.5 percent. Cross-link Part 19.0 ¬ßPMTUD case study.

**Synthetic exercise.**

```bash
sudo ovn-nbctl lr-add lr-frag
sudo ovn-nbctl lrp-add lr-frag lrp-pub 02:ac:10:ff:00:01 192.168.50.1/24
sudo ovn-nbctl ls-add ls-pub
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-add ls-pub lsp-lrp-pub
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-set-type lsp-lrp-pub router
sudo ovn-nbctl lsp-set-options lsp-lrp-pub router-port=lrp-pub
sudo ovn-nbctl lb-add lb-frag "10.10.0.100:443" "10.0.0.10:443,10.0.0.11:443"
sudo ovn-nbctl lr-lb-add lr-frag lb-frag
# Lab task 1: ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr-frag | grep lr_in_defrag should show two flows at priorities 100 and 0.
# Lab task 2: use scapy to send an IPv4 packet split into 3 fragments to VIP 10.10.0.100:443.
# Lab task 3: dpctl/dump-conntrack zone=<lr_zone> should show the entry with the original src (not the VIP).
# Lab task 4: lower ipfrag_time to 5s, send fragments 6s apart, and observe the drop.
# Lab task 5: tcpdump br-int to observe that the post-reassembly packet has the full L4 header.
```

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.**
- Mode A "reassembly timeout": fragments 2 to N arrive later than `ipfrag_time`. The counter `cat /proc/net/snmp | grep ReasmFails` rises. Increase the timeout.
- Mode B "`nf_defrag` module not loaded": `lsmod | grep nf_defrag` is empty. Run `modprobe nf_defrag_ipv4 nf_defrag_ipv6`.
- Mode C "memory threshold exhaustion": `ipfrag_high_thresh` is too low for a fragment burst. Read `/proc/sys/net/ipv4/ipfrag_high_thresh` and raise it to 8 MB.
- Mode D "logical stage id shifts after an upgrade": OVN 23.06 adds DEFRAG_ECMP_STATEFUL, so the OF table number shifts. Update monitoring scripts to grep "lr_in_defrag" instead of hardcoding table=13.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux iptables: `iptables -t raw -A PREROUTING -m frag --first` does not actually reassemble; it only matches fragments. Reassembly is done by `nf_defrag_ipv4.ko` automatically when conntrack is enabled. OVN wraps the same kernel module behind the `ct_next` action more explicitly. NSX-T DLR has implicit IP defrag when the LB is enabled, but does not expose it as a separate stage. Cisco IOS XE virtual IP reassembly (`ip virtual-reassembly`) is semantically equivalent; it is activated through interface configuration rather than as a pipeline stage.

---

## ¬ß13.19.17 LR_IN_UNSNAT, reverse SNAT for reply packets entering the router

**Concept.** `LR_IN_UNSNAT` is the Logical_Router ingress logical stage responsible for **un-SNAT (reverse SNAT)**: it receives reply packets from the external network whose destination IP matches the external IP of a SNAT entry, and rewrites the destination back to the original internal IP before routing. The reverse direction of `LR_OUT_SNAT` (forward SNAT on egress). Type: **stateful NAT reverser** at the medium tier, subtype "ingress reverse SNAT".

**History and background.** The stage has existed since early OVN versions (OVN 2.6, 2017), when masquerade-style NAT rules were added by Numan Siddique. Before this stage, reply packets to the external IP had no mechanism to translate back to the internal IP, so connections from internal hosts to the Internet could not receive replies. UNSNAT solves this through conntrack reverse mapping. OVN 21.03 added the distributed gateway port, allowing UNSNAT to run distributed (each chassis has a local SNAT entry). OVN 22.03 stabilised the design.

**Position in the architecture.** Logical stage 4 maps to OF table 12. It follows `LR_IN_DEFRAG_ECMP_STATEFUL` (when present, OVN 23.06+) or `LR_IN_IP_INPUT` (logical 3) in 22.03. It precedes `LR_IN_DEFRAG` (logical 5). Part 13.16 line 240 explains the ordering: UNSNAT comes before DEFRAG because reply packets arrive with the SNAT-translated destination and must be un-SNAT-ed first, after which DEFRAG runs and DNAT lookup works correctly. Scope: per-packet, per-LR datapath, only packets whose destination matches a SNAT external_ip.

**Role.** Main role: **rewrite the destination IP from the external SNAT IP back to the original internal IP** for reply packets. Secondary role: invoke `ct(commit, nat)` in the NAT zone so that the kernel conntrack looks up the old mapping. Scope: only packets entering the router (ingress); the forward path is not affected.

**Why it exists.** In a multi-tenant setup, many internal subnets share a small number of external IPs (the masquerade pattern). Egress SNAT translates the source to the external IP and conntrack stores the mapping. When the reply arrives with destination equal to the external IP, the OVS kernel conntrack can find the reverse entry, but the OVN pipeline needs an explicit stage to (a) match the destination IP against a SNAT rule's external_ip, (b) call reverse `nat()`, (c) ensure the state is ESTABLISHED. UNSNAT carves out this stage before routing so that the destination IP is correct when `LR_IN_IP_ROUTING` resolves the next hop.

**Problems it solves.** Specifically: (a) reply packets arrive with destination set to the external SNAT IP, (b) the destination must be translated back to the original internal IP, (c) the conntrack reverse mapping must still be alive. Solution: a priority 90 flow that matches `ip4.dst == <external_ip> && inport == "<gateway-port>"` and runs `ct(commit, zone=<nat_zone>, nat)` (the NAT module reverses when it finds the entry). Scope: only stateful SNAT with an ESTABLISHED conntrack entry. If the entry expires (timeout), the packet becomes INVALID and is dropped by a downstream ACL stage.

**Importance.** Medium. Required for every LR that has a SNAT rule (both masquerade and dnat_and_snat types). If disabled, internal VMs cannot receive replies from the Internet. Operator essential: understand the UNSNAT, then DEFRAG, then DNAT order when debugging "the VM can ping the Internet but does not receive a reply".

**How it works.** The flow generator `build_lrouter_in_unsnat_flow` (`northd/northd.c`, branch-22.03) iterates the NBDB `NAT` table. For each entry with `type==snat` or `type==dnat_and_snat` it emits the flow:

```
table=12 (lr_in_unsnat), priority=90,
  match=(ip && ip4.dst == 192.0.2.10 && inport == "lrp-pub"),
  action=(ct(commit, nat, zone=<nat_zone>);)

table=12 (lr_in_unsnat), priority=0,
  match=(1), action=(next;)
```

Different from `LR_OUT_SNAT` in that this stage does not need a `nat(src=...)` parameter. The argumentless `nat` action automatically uses the reverse-direction entry already present in conntrack. The controller translates this to OVS `ct(commit, zone=NXM_NX_REG11[0..15], nat)`. Invariant: after this stage, the packet destination IP has been rewritten by the kernel if the conntrack entry exists; otherwise the destination remains external and the packet will fall through normal routing (typically rejected).

**How an operator masters this.** (1) **Read**: look for priority 90 flows in OF table 12 with `ip.dst==<external>` to identify which SNAT rules are active. (2) **Modify**: `ovn-nbctl lr-nat-add lr-pub snat 192.0.2.10 10.0.0.0/24` to add a masquerade rule. (3) **Observe**: `ovs-appctl dpctl/dump-conntrack zone=<nat_zone> | grep <external_ip>` to see the mapping entry. (4) **Debug**: if the reply does not reach the VM, use `ofproto/trace 'in_port=N,ip,ip_dst=<external>,ct_state=+est+rpl'` to verify that stage 12 rewrites the destination.

**Classification.** Stage type: **stateful NAT reverser** (subtype "ingress reverse SNAT"). Different from `LR_IN_DNAT` (forward DNAT, logical stage 6) and from `LR_OUT_SNAT` (forward SNAT, egress). Same NAT family as `LR_OUT_UNDNAT` (egress reverse DNAT, the symmetric counterpart of UNSNAT in the opposite direction).

**Usage workflow.** When configuring masquerade SNAT:
1. Create the LR and gateway port: `ovn-nbctl lr-add lr-pub && ovn-nbctl lrp-add lr-pub lrp-pub 02:ac:10:ff:00:01 192.0.2.1/24`.
2. Set the gateway chassis: `ovn-nbctl lrp-set-gateway-chassis lrp-pub chassis-1 priority=10`.
3. Add the SNAT rule: `ovn-nbctl lr-nat-add lr-pub snat 192.0.2.10 10.0.0.0/24`.
4. Verify the ingress UNSNAT flow: `ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr-pub | grep lr_in_unsnat` should show flows at priorities 90 and 0.
5. Test: from the internal VM `10.0.0.5`, curl the Internet. The NAT conntrack zone should have an entry; the reply should show the destination rewritten from `192.0.2.10` to `10.0.0.5` at stage 12.

**When something goes wrong.** Typical symptoms: (a) "the internal VM sends ICMP requests to the Internet but no reply arrives", which suggests a UNSNAT miss or an expired conntrack entry. (b) "TCP connections idle longer than 5 minutes are dropped", which suggests `tcp_timeout_established` is too short. Diagnostic:
- Step 1: `ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr-pub | grep lr_in_unsnat` to confirm the flow exists.
- Step 2: `ovs-appctl dpctl/dump-conntrack zone=<nat_zone>` to view the SNAT entry.
- Step 3: use `ofproto/trace` on a synthetic reply packet and check whether stage 12 rewrites the destination.
- Step 4: if the trace looks correct but the VM still does not receive the packet, check whether a downstream egress ACL stage drops it.

Cross-link Part 13.3 ¬ßNAT, Part 19.0 ¬ßlong-running connection timeout case.

**Tightly related to.** Tightly coupled: the NBDB `NAT` table (columns `type`, `external_ip`, `logical_ip`), `LR_OUT_SNAT` (the corresponding forward path), `LR_OUT_UNDNAT` (the egress symmetric counterpart of UNSNAT), the dedicated NAT conntrack zone (not shared with the routing zone), `LR_IN_DEFRAG` (the downstream consumer that needs the correct destination), and the kernel `nf_nat_module`. Loose: `LR_IN_IP_ROUTING` (downstream consumer once the packet has been un-SNAT-ed).

**Version differences.**
- OVN 2.6 (2017): introduces the SNAT and DNAT NAT types with the basic UNSNAT stage.
- OVN 20.06: adds support for the `dnat_and_snat` type (floating IP).
- OVN 21.03: distributed gateway port lets UNSNAT run locally on each chassis.
- OVN 22.03 (curriculum baseline): the NAT zone is separated from the routing zone.
- OVN 23.06: adds `LR_IN_DEFRAG_ECMP_STATEFUL` inserted before UNSNAT for ECMP stateful.
- OVN 24.03: optimisation, combines UNSNAT with UNDNAT when the rule is `dnat_and_snat`.

**C√°ch quan s√°t + x√°c minh.**

```
$ ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr-pub | grep -A2 "lr_in_unsnat"
  table=4 (lr_in_unsnat), priority=90,
    match=(ip && ip4.dst == 192.0.2.10 && inport == "lrp-pub"),
    action=(ct(commit, nat, zone=8001);)
  table=4 (lr_in_unsnat), priority=0,
    match=(1), action=(next;)

$ ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int table=12 | head -3
  cookie=0x2, table=12, n_packets=128, ip,nw_dst=192.0.2.10,
  metadata=0x2, actions=ct(commit,zone=NXM_NX_REG11[0..15],nat)

$ ovs-appctl dpctl/dump-conntrack zone=8001 | grep 192.0.2.10
  tcp,orig=(src=10.0.0.5,dst=8.8.8.8,sport=4001,dport=443),
       reply=(src=8.8.8.8,dst=192.0.2.10,sport=443,dport=4001),
       protoinfo=(state=ESTABLISHED), zone=8001
```

**Source code tham chiŠļŅu.**
- `northd/northd.c` `build_lrouter_in_unsnat_flow()`: the function that emits flows for this stage (verified at branch-22.03).
- `lib/actions.c` parser for `ct(commit, nat)` without arguments.
- `controller/lflow.c` translates `ct(nat)` to OVS with zone REG11.
- `northd/ovn-northd.8.xml` ¬ß"Ingress Table 4: UNSNAT" documentation.
- `ovn-nb.ovsschema` table `NAT`, columns `external_ip`, `logical_ip`, and `type`.
- Kernel: `net/netfilter/nf_nat_core.c` `nf_nat_setup_info()` reverse mapping.
- Pre-existing reference: Part 13.16 line 220, Part 13.3 ¬ßNAT type catalog.

**Real-world incident.** Q2 2024, an OpenStack production cluster. The customer used masquerade SNAT for 200 VMs sharing a single floating IP gateway. The report: "long-running TCP connections (database replication, idle for over an hour) get reset at random". Investigation: `dpctl/dump-conntrack zone=<nat_zone>` showed the connections still in ESTABLISHED state. `ofproto/trace` on a reply packet looked correct, with UNSNAT matching priority 90. Root cause: the kernel `tcp_timeout_established` default of 432000s (5 days) was fine, but the OVS userspace `tcp_seq_chk_timeout` of 7200s (2 hours) lined up with the long-idle replication pattern. Fix: `ovs-vsctl set Open_vSwitch . other_config:tcp-loose=true` to allow loose tracking; move database replication onto a separate LR that does not go through NAT. Cross-link Part 13.3 ¬ßNAT timeout, Part 19.0 ¬ßdatabase replication case.

**Synthetic exercise.**

```bash
sudo ovn-nbctl lr-add lr-snat
sudo ovn-nbctl lrp-add lr-snat lrp-pub 02:ac:10:ff:00:01 192.0.2.1/24
sudo ovn-nbctl lrp-set-gateway-chassis lrp-pub chassis-1 priority=10
sudo ovn-nbctl ls-add ls-int
sudo ovn-nbctl lrp-add lr-snat lrp-int 02:ac:10:00:00:01 10.0.0.1/24
sudo ovn-nbctl lr-nat-add lr-snat snat 192.0.2.10 10.0.0.0/24
# Lab task 1: ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr-snat | grep lr_in_unsnat should show a priority 90 flow.
# Lab task 2: from VM 10.0.0.5, ping 8.8.8.8 (via external). tcpdump on the uplink should show src=192.0.2.10.
# Lab task 3: ofproto/trace simulating a reply: in_port=lrp-pub, dst=192.0.2.10, ct_state=+est+rpl.
# Lab task 4: delete the SNAT rule (lr-nat-del) and verify the flow disappears and the reply is no longer un-SNAT-ed.
# Lab task 5: simulate conntrack timeout: sysctl -w net.netfilter.nf_conntrack_tcp_timeout_established=10 and observe that connections older than 10s are reset.
```

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.**
- Mode A "reply not un-SNAT-ed": the priority 90 flow does not match because `ip.dst` is wrong. Check that the `NAT` row's `external_ip` is correct.
- Mode B "idle connection is reset": the conntrack timeout is shorter than the idle time. Increase `nf_conntrack_tcp_timeout_established`.
- Mode C "reply goes to the wrong VM": multiple SNAT rules have overlapping external IPs. Enforce uniqueness on `NAT.external_ip`.
- Mode D "INVALID state on the reply": conntrack zone collision (the routing zone overlaps the NAT zone). Check the ovn-northd zone allocation.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux iptables: `iptables -t nat -A POSTROUTING -j MASQUERADE` for the egress direction; the reply is reversed automatically by conntrack (`-m conntrack --ctstate ESTABLISHED,RELATED -j ACCEPT`). OVN factors UNSNAT into an explicit pipeline stage, which is clearer than the iptables implicit form. Cisco ASA NAT uses `nat (inside,outside) source dynamic any interface`; the reply is un-NAT-ed automatically without exposing a stage. NSX-T DLR uplink NAT is semantically equivalent and distributed.

---

## ¬ß13.19.18 LR_IN_LARGER_PKTS, ICMP frag-needed for oversized packets

**Concept.** `LR_IN_LARGER_PKTS` is the Logical_Router ingress logical stage that detects packets **larger than the MTU** of the next-hop link and responds to the source with an ICMP "Destination Unreachable, Fragmentation Needed and Don't Fragment Set" (ICMP type 3 code 4 for IPv4) or "Packet Too Big" (ICMPv6 type 2 for IPv6). Purpose: support Path MTU Discovery (PMTUD) for clients connecting through a tunnel whose MTU is smaller than the LAN MTU. Type: **PMTUD enforcer** at the medium tier, subtype "ICMP error generator".

**History and background.** The stage was added by Lorenzo Bianconi and Numan Siddique in OVN 21.03 (2021) when the distributed gateway port first faced Internet traffic over a Geneve tunnel. Geneve has 50 bytes of overhead, so the effective MTU is 1450 if the underlay MTU is 1500. A client sending a 1500-byte packet with DF=1 silently drops, and PMTUD fails. Before 21.03, OVN did not emit an ICMP response, so the client had no signal to reduce MSS. Lorenzo added the `check_pkt_larger` action upstream in OVS commit `fd7f64bb98` (2021-01), and OVN integrated it into the pipeline.

**Position in the architecture.** Logical stage 17 maps to OF table 25. It follows `LR_IN_CHK_PKT_LEN` (logical 16, OF table 24) and precedes `LR_IN_GW_REDIRECT` (logical 18, OF table 26 in the 22.03 baseline; Part 13.16 line 1116 documents GW_REDIRECT as logical 18 mapping to OF 26, so LARGER_PKTS sits in between). Scope: per-packet, per-LR datapath, only packets that `LR_IN_CHK_PKT_LEN` has marked in `REGBIT_PKT_LARGER`.

**Role.** Main role: **emit an ICMP frag-needed back to the source**, with the valid old MTU in the `nexthop MTU` field. Secondary role: drop the original packet (after the ICMP is emitted) so that an oversized packet does not propagate. Scope: only packets with `eth_type=ip` or `ipv6` and `REGBIT_PKT_LARGER == 1`. Normal packets fall through to priority 0 `next`.

**Why it exists.** Pre-existence pain: in environments with a Geneve tunnel (50 bytes of overlay), when a client sends a 1460-byte TCP segment with DF=1, the outer IP packet is 1500 bytes, the tunnel-encapsulated packet is 1550 bytes, and it exceeds the underlay MTU of 1500. The kernel drops it at hypervisor ingress. The client does not receive an ICMP response (the underlay drops do not generate ICMP), TCP retries three times, and the connection blackholes. This is the classic "MTU blackhole" PMTUD failure. OVN solves it with two stages: `LR_IN_CHK_PKT_LEN` measures the size and sets a REGBIT, and `LR_IN_LARGER_PKTS` emits the ICMP. The client receives ICMP type 3 code 4, lowers its MSS cache, retries with a smaller segment, and traffic gets through.

**Problems it solves.** Specifically: (a) packets larger than the next-hop MTU with DF=1, (b) the need to respond with ICMP so the client learns the correct MTU, (c) the need to drop the original packet. Solution: a priority 50 flow that matches `eth.dst == <LRP_MAC> && reg9[1] == 1` (REGBIT_PKT_LARGER), with the action `icmp4 { eth.dst <-> eth.src; ip4.dst = ip4.src; ip4.src = <LRP_IP>; ip.ttl = 254; icmp4.type = 3; icmp4.code = 4; next; }; output;` for IPv4, and the equivalent `icmp6` for IPv6. Scope: only DF=1 packets that exceed the next-hop MTU. UDP and non-DF packets are fragmented by the kernel at the IP layer and never reach this stage.

**Importance.** Medium. Required for every LR that faces Internet or tunnel traffic. Without this stage the customer sees "web pages load slowly at random" or "file downloads stuck at 99 percent". Operator essential: understand the pair `LR_IN_CHK_PKT_LEN` (sets the bit) and `LR_IN_LARGER_PKTS` (emits the ICMP) as the PMTUD mechanism inside the OVN pipeline.

**How it works.** The flow generator is `build_lrouter_force_pkt_larger_flows` (`northd/northd.c`, branch-22.03). The action `check_pkt_larger(MTU)` parses in `lib/actions.c` to `ofpact_check_pkt_larger`. The controller translates it to the OVS action `check_pkt_larger:<mtu>->reg9[1]`. The CHK_PKT_LEN stage uses this action to set the bit; the LARGER_PKTS stage reads it:

```
table=25 (lr_in_larger_pkts), priority=50,
  match=(inport == "lrp-pub" && ip4 && reg9[1] == 1),
  action=(icmp4 {
            eth.dst <-> eth.src;
            ip4.dst = ip4.src;
            ip4.src = 192.0.2.1;
            ip.ttl = 254;
            icmp4.type = 3;
            icmp4.code = 4;   /* fragmentation needed */
            icmp4.payload = orig_packet[0..63];
            next;
          };
          output;)

table=25 (lr_in_larger_pkts), priority=0,
  match=(1), action=(next;)
```

The action `icmp4 { ... }` parses to a nested action set in `lib/actions.c`. The controller translates it to a multi-step OVS action sequence using `learn` to build the ICMP packet in place. Invariant: after this stage, the original packet drops (the ICMP is output and there is no resubmit), and the client receives an ICMP with the correct MTU in the unused field (typically 1450 with Geneve).

**How an operator masters this.** (1) **Read**: understand that `reg9[1] == 1` is the REGBIT_PKT_LARGER check set by CHK_PKT_LEN. (2) **Modify**: configure `Logical_Router_Port.options:gateway_mtu` per LRP so the pipeline knows the next-hop MTU. (3) **Observe**: `tcpdump -i <iface_external>` should show ICMP type 3 code 4 responses. (4) **Debug**: if PMTUD fails, check whether `gateway_mtu` is set on the LR and whether the REGBIT is set at the CHK_PKT_LEN stage.

**Classification.** Stage type: **PMTUD enforcer** (subtype "ICMP error generator"). Different from `LR_IN_CHK_PKT_LEN` (size detector that sets the bit) and from `LR_IN_IP_INPUT` (handles local self-traffic ICMP and does not emit ICMP errors for the forward path). Same family as the emit-and-drop pattern (`LR_IN_ARP_REQUEST`).

**Usage workflow.** When the Geneve tunnel MTU is smaller than the client LAN MTU:
1. Set the MTU per LRP: `ovn-nbctl set Logical_Router_Port lrp-pub options:gateway_mtu=1450`.
2. Verify the CHK_PKT_LEN flow: `ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr-pub | grep lr_in_chk_pkt_len`.
3. Verify the LARGER_PKTS flow: `ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr-pub | grep lr_in_larger_pkts` should show a priority 50 flow.
4. Test PMTUD: from an external client, send `ping -s 1500 -M do <VIP>`. Expect an ICMP type 3 code 4 response.
5. Verify TCP MSS clamping: a TCP connection through the VIP with a SYN packet should show the MSS option automatically reduced to 1410 (1450 minus 40 bytes of header).

**When something goes wrong.** Typical symptoms: (a) "the web client connects to the VIP but the page hangs in the loading state", which suggests an MTU blackhole and that ICMP frag-needed is blocked by a firewall on the path. (b) "downloads of files larger than 1 MB get stuck at random", which suggests the segment size exceeds the path MTU and no ICMP is received. Diagnostic:
- Step 1: capture on the LR uplink with `tcpdump -ni eth0 'icmp[0]==3 and icmp[1]==4'` to check whether ICMP is sent.
- Step 2: if ICMP is sent but the client does not receive it, a firewall on the path is blocking ICMP type 3. Work around with TCP MSS clamping at the client.
- Step 3: if the LR does not send the ICMP, check whether the `gateway_mtu` option is set.
- Step 4: use `ofproto/trace` to check whether REGBIT_PKT_LARGER is set at the CHK_PKT_LEN stage.

Cross-link Part 19.0 ¬ßPMTUD blackhole, Part 11.0 ¬ßGeneve overhead.

**Tightly related to.** Tightly coupled: `LR_IN_CHK_PKT_LEN` (upstream stage that sets REGBIT_PKT_LARGER), `REGBIT_PKT_LARGER = reg9[1]` (the oversized-packet flag), `Logical_Router_Port.options:gateway_mtu` (the MTU configuration source), the OVS `check_pkt_larger` action, and the nested `icmp4{}` and `icmp6{}` actions. Loose: TCP MSS clamping at the client (an alternative when PMTUD fails).

**Version differences.**
- OVN 21.03 (2021): introduces the stage along with the `check_pkt_larger` action.
- OVN 21.06: adds support for IPv6 ICMP "Packet Too Big".
- OVN 22.03 (baseline): stable, with the full `icmp4{}` action template.
- OVN 23.03: adds `gateway_mtu_bypass` support to bypass for specific traffic classes (for example, DHCP).
- OVN 24.03: optimises `check_pkt_larger` for the kernel datapath through the `pkt_size` action.

**C√°ch quan s√°t + x√°c minh.**

```
$ ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr-pub | grep -A6 "lr_in_larger_pkts"
  table=17 (lr_in_larger_pkts), priority=50,
    match=(inport == "lrp-pub" && ip4 && reg9[1] == 1),
    action=(icmp4 { eth.dst <-> eth.src; ip4.dst = ip4.src;
            ip4.src = 192.0.2.1; ip.ttl = 254;
            icmp4.type = 3; icmp4.code = 4; next; }; output;)

$ ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int table=25 | head -3
  cookie=0x3, table=25, n_packets=8, ip,reg9=0x2/0x2,
  metadata=0x3, actions=...

$ tcpdump -ni eth-pub 'icmp[0]==3 and icmp[1]==4' -c 2
  IP 192.0.2.1 > 1.2.3.4: ICMP 192.0.2.10 unreachable - need to frag (mtu 1450), length 36
```

**Source code reference.**
- `northd/northd.c` `build_lrouter_force_pkt_larger_flows()`: the function that emits flows for this stage (verified at branch-22.03).
- `lib/actions.c` parser for the nested `icmp4{}` action set.
- `lib/ofp-actions.c` `check_pkt_larger` action (OVS upstream commit `fd7f64bb98`, 2021-01).
- `controller/lflow.c` translates the nested `icmp4` action to a multi-step OVS action.
- `northd/ovn-northd.8.xml` ¬ß"Ingress Table 17: Larger packets" documentation.
- `ovn-nb.ovsschema` `Logical_Router_Port` column `options:gateway_mtu`.
- Pre-existing reference: Part 13.16 line 233, Part 11.0 ¬ßGeneve MTU overhead.

**Real-world incident.** Q1 2025, an OpenStack production cluster with OVN 22.03. The customer migrated from legacy DVR to the OVN distributed gateway. After migration, mobile app users reported "image upload fails at random for 30 percent of attempts". Investigation: tcpdump on the gateway chassis showed 1460-byte TCP segments arriving but no ICMP frag-needed response. `ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr-pub | grep lr_in_larger_pkts` was empty. Root cause: after the migration, the customer forgot to set the `gateway_mtu` option on the LRP. By default OVN does not generate CHK_PKT_LEN or LARGER_PKTS flows when `gateway_mtu` is unset. Fix: `ovn-nbctl set Logical_Router_Port lrp-pub options:gateway_mtu=1450`. ovn-northd auto-recompiled, the flow appeared within two seconds, and PMTUD resumed. Cross-link Part 19.0 ¬ßmigration PMTUD case.

**Synthetic exercise.**

```bash
sudo ovn-nbctl lr-add lr-mtu
sudo ovn-nbctl lrp-add lr-mtu lrp-pub 02:ac:10:ff:00:01 192.0.2.1/24
sudo ovn-nbctl set Logical_Router_Port lrp-pub options:gateway_mtu=1450
sudo ovn-nbctl lrp-set-gateway-chassis lrp-pub chassis-1 priority=10
# Lab task 1: ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr-mtu | grep lr_in_larger_pkts should show a priority 50 flow.
# Lab task 2: from an external host, send `ping -s 1500 -M do 192.0.2.10`. Expect ICMP type 3 code 4.
# Lab task 3: tcpdump on the external uplink with filter 'icmp[0]==3' counts the ICMPs sent.
# Lab task 4: remove the gateway_mtu option and verify that the LARGER_PKTS flow disappears and `ping -s 1500` receives no ICMP.
# Lab task 5: test IPv6 ICMP6 type 2 with gateway_mtu on an IPv6 LRP.
```

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.**
- Mode A "LARGER_PKTS flow does not generate": `gateway_mtu` is unset. Run `ovn-nbctl set Logical_Router_Port lrp options:gateway_mtu=1450`.
- Mode B "ICMP is sent correctly but the client does not receive it": a firewall on the path blocks ICMP type 3. Work around with TCP MSS clamping `ovs-ofctl add-flow br-int 'tcp,tcp_flags=+syn,actions=mod_tp_dst:1410,...'`.
- Mode C "REGBIT_PKT_LARGER is not set": the CHK_PKT_LEN stage does not match. Check that `eth.dst == LRP_MAC` is correct.
- Mode D "wrong MTU value in the ICMP": the `gateway_mtu` option has the wrong number (for example 1500 instead of 1450). Recompute the Geneve overhead correctly.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux kernel routing automatically emits ICMP frag-needed when a packet exceeds the outgoing interface MTU (DF=1) through `icmp_send()` in `net/ipv4/route.c`. OVN replicates this logic at the OF pipeline level because packets in the OVN pipeline have not yet passed through the main kernel routing. Cisco IOS XE: the `ip mtu` command on an interface automatically triggers ICMP frag-needed. NSX-T DLR supports PMTUD in the kernel datapath without exposing a pipeline stage. AWS Transit Gateway has implicit PMTUD support, configured per attachment MTU.

---

## ¬ß13.19.19 LR_IN_ND_RA_OPTIONS, IPv6 Neighbor Discovery and Router Advertisement

**Concept.** `LR_IN_ND_RA_OPTIONS` is the Logical_Router ingress logical stage that **builds IPv6 RA (Router Advertisement) options** when an ICMPv6 Router Solicitation arrives from a host in a connected subnet, and builds ND (Neighbor Discovery) replies for ND queries. Purpose: replace the kernel `radvd` daemon. OVN handles RS/RA and NS/NA inside the pipeline to support SLAAC (Stateless Address Auto-Configuration) for IPv6 VMs. Type: **IPv6 ND/RA responder** at the medium tier, subtype "control plane ICMPv6 emitter".

**History and background.** The stage was introduced by Mark Michelson and Numan Siddique in OVN 20.06 (2020), when IPv6 SLAAC support was first completed. Before that, IPv6 in OVN required static configuration (manual IPv6 on each VM). SLAAC needs an entity that emits RAs periodically and answers RSs. The external `radvd` is awkward when the LR is distributed: each chassis needs its own radvd with synchronised configuration. OVN integrates this stage internally to support distributed RA: each chassis runs ovn-controller, which installs local flows to answer RS locally without needing an extra daemon.

**Position in the architecture.** Logical stage 8 maps to OF table 16. It follows `LR_IN_DNAT` (logical 6) and precedes the core routing stages (`LR_IN_LOOKUP_NEIGHBOR` logical 9 onward). Confirmed at Part 13.16 line 224. Scope: per-packet, per-LR datapath. Only ICMPv6 type 133 (Router Solicitation) or 135 (Neighbor Solicitation) packets match the high priority here; IPv4 packets and other ICMPv6 packets fall through to priority 0 `next`.

**Role.** Main role: **emit an ICMPv6 RA reply** with the full option set (Source Link-Layer Address, MTU, Prefix Information) for a host's Router Solicitation. Secondary role: emit an ICMPv6 NA for a Neighbor Solicitation that queries the LRP address (the router IP). Scope: control plane IPv6 ND and RA only. No data forwarding.

**Why it exists.** Before OVN 20.06, VM IPv6 SLAAC required the admin to run a `radvd` daemon on the gateway chassis with a manual configuration file containing the prefix for each LS. In a multi-tenant environment with about 100 LSes, the radvd configuration grew long and error-prone to keep in sync. When the LR distributed gateway port arrived, each chassis needed its own local radvd, leading to a configuration explosion and synchronisation overhead. OVN integrates the ND/RA response into the pipeline declaratively: the admin only sets `lrp-set-options ipv6_ra_configs:address_mode=slaac`, and ovn-northd generates the RS-answer flow and the periodic RA emit on its own.

**Problems it solves.** Specifically: (a) IPv6 VMs need RAs to auto-configure their address and default route, (b) NS queries for the LRP address need an NA reply so the VM learns the LRP MAC, (c) the periodic RA emit (default 200s interval) needs a timer mechanism. Solution: a priority 80 flow that matches `nd_rs && eth.dst == <multicast all-routers FF02::2>` and runs `put_nd_ra_opts(...)` (which builds the RA option set from the `ipv6_ra_configs` LRP options) and emits the reply directly back to the source. Periodic RA: ovn-controller has a scheduled mechanism to emit an RA every `max_interval` seconds through the `nd_ra` schedule. Scope: SLAAC and stateless DHCPv6. Stateful DHCPv6 with an assigned address pool requires an external DHCPv6 server.

**Importance.** Medium. Required for every LR that hosts IPv6 SLAAC VMs. If disabled, IPv6 VMs cannot auto-configure addresses and fall back to link-local only. Operator essential: understand that this stage is where OVN replaces the radvd daemon, and know the `ipv6_ra_configs` option to configure prefix and interval.

**How it works.** The flow generator is `build_lrouter_in_nd_ra_options` (`northd/northd.c`, branch-22.03; OVN 22.03 has the `build_ND_RA_options` helper). The action `put_nd_ra_opts(addr_mode=slaac, prefix=..., mtu=..., source_lla=...)` parses in `lib/actions.c` to `ofpact_put_nd_ra_opts`, and the controller translates it to a multi-step OVS action sequence that builds the ICMPv6 packet from scratch. Template flow:

```
table=16 (lr_in_nd_ra_options), priority=80,
  match=(inport == "lrp-int" && nd_rs && ip6.dst == ff02::2),
  action=(eth.dst <-> eth.src; ip6.dst = ip6.src;
          ip6.src = fe80::21:0:0:1;   /* LRP link-local */
          nd_ra.flags = 0xc0;          /* M=0, O=1, prf=high */
          put_nd_ra_opts(addr_mode = "slaac",
                         router_lifetime = 1800,
                         mtu = 1450,
                         prefix = 2001:db8:1::/64,
                         valid_lifetime = 86400,
                         preferred_lifetime = 14400);
          next;
          output;)

table=16 (lr_in_nd_ra_options), priority=0,
  match=(1), action=(next;)
```

Periodic (unsolicited) RAs are scheduled by ovn-controller through the `pinctrl` thread every `max_interval` seconds (default 200s). Invariant: every IPv6 VM joining an LS receives an RA within `max_interval` seconds, or sooner if it actively sends an RS.

**How an operator masters this.** (1) **Read**: understand the RA option set through `nd_ra.flags`, prefix lifetime, and MTU. (2) **Modify**: `ovn-nbctl lrp-set-options lrp-int ipv6_ra_configs:address_mode=slaac ipv6_ra_configs:prefix=2001:db8:1::/64`. (3) **Observe**: `tcpdump -ni vnet0 'icmp6 and ip6[40]==134'` (type 134 is RA) on the VM that receives RAs. (4) **Debug**: if the VM cannot auto-configure IPv6, check whether `ipv6_ra_configs` is set and whether the stage flow is generated.

**Classification.** Stage type: **control plane responder** (subtype "IPv6 ND/RA emitter"). Different from `LS_IN_ARP_ND_RSP` (LS level, answers NS for non-router IPs, LS stage 17) and from `LR_IN_IP_INPUT` (handles ICMP echo, does not build RA options). Same family as the DHCP responder stages (`LS_IN_DHCP_OPTIONS`, `LS_IN_DHCP_RESPONSE`).

**Usage workflow.** Configuring IPv6 SLAAC:
1. Create the LR and IPv6 LRP: `ovn-nbctl lrp-add lr-int lrp-int 02:ac:10:00:00:01 2001:db8:1::1/64`.
2. Set the RA configuration: `ovn-nbctl lrp-set-options lrp-int ipv6_ra_configs:address_mode=slaac ipv6_ra_configs:prefix=2001:db8:1::/64 ipv6_ra_configs:max_interval=200 ipv6_ra_configs:mtu=1450`.
3. Verify the ND_RA_OPTIONS flow: `ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr-int | grep lr_in_nd_ra_options` should show a priority 80 flow.
4. Create the LS and VM: `ovn-nbctl ls-add ls-int && ovn-nbctl lsp-add ls-int lsp-vm-ipv6`.
5. Test SLAAC: on the VM, `ip -6 addr show eth0` should show an auto-configured address `2001:db8:1::xxxx/64` and a default route through `fe80::21:0:0:1`.

**When something goes wrong.** Typical symptoms: (a) "the IPv6 VM has only the link-local fe80:: address and no global address", which suggests SLAAC failed and no RA reached the VM. (b) "the VM loses IPv6 connectivity after 30 minutes", which suggests the RA lifetime expired without a refresh. Diagnostic:
- Step 1: `tcpdump -ni vnet0 icmp6` on the VM checks whether RAs are received.
- Step 2: if not, `ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr-int | grep lr_in_nd_ra_options` checks whether the flow is generated.
- Step 3: if the flow is empty, check `Logical_Router_Port.options:ipv6_ra_configs` with `ovn-nbctl get lrp-int options`.
- Step 4: if the flow exists but no RA arrives, check the periodic timer in ovn-controller with `ovs-appctl -t ovn-controller debug/status`.
- Step 5: if only refresh is missing, lower `max_interval` to 60s or shorten `valid_lifetime` for a higher rate.

Cross-link Part 18.0 ¬ßIPv6 ND, Part 13.10 ¬ßSLAAC.

**Tightly related to.** Tightly coupled: `Logical_Router_Port.options:ipv6_ra_configs` (the configuration map that is the source of RA content), the `put_nd_ra_opts` action (`lib/actions.c`), the periodic `nd_ra` action (scheduled by the controller), `LS_IN_ARP_ND_RSP` (the LS-level stage that answers NS from other VMs in the same LS), and the `pinctrl` thread in ovn-controller (responsible for the periodic emit). Loose: the `radvd` daemon (the external alternative that OVN replaces).

**Version differences.**
- OVN 20.06 (2020): introduces the stage along with the `put_nd_ra_opts` action.
- OVN 20.12: adds support for stateless DHCPv6 coexisting with SLAAC.
- OVN 21.06: makes `min_interval` and `router_lifetime` configurable.
- OVN 22.03 (baseline): stable; adds the `mtu` option in the RA.
- OVN 23.03: adds DNS/RDNSS options in the RA for recursive DNS.
- OVN 24.03: optimises periodic emit with a `meter` to rate-limit RA emission.

**C√°ch quan s√°t + x√°c minh.**

```
$ ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr-int | grep -A8 "lr_in_nd_ra_options"
  table=8 (lr_in_nd_ra_options), priority=80,
    match=(inport == "lrp-int" && nd_rs && ip6.dst == ff02::2),
    action=(eth.dst <-> eth.src; ip6.dst = ip6.src;
            ip6.src = fe80::21:0:0:1;
            nd_ra.flags = 0xc0;
            put_nd_ra_opts(addr_mode = "slaac",
                           prefix = 2001:db8:1::/64, mtu = 1450);
            next;
            output;)

$ tcpdump -ni vnet-vm 'icmp6 and ip6[40]==134' -c 1
  IP6 fe80::21:0:0:1 > ff02::1: ICMP6, router advertisement, length 64
    M=0 O=1 lifetime=1800 prefix=2001:db8:1::/64 mtu=1450

$ ip -6 addr show eth0   # tr√™n VM
  inet6 2001:db8:1::5054:ff:fe00:0001/64 scope global dynamic mngtmpaddr
  inet6 fe80::5054:ff:fe00:0001/64 scope link
```

**Source code reference.**
- `northd/northd.c` `build_lrouter_in_nd_ra_options()`: the function that emits flows for this stage (verified at branch-22.03).
- `lib/actions.c` parser for the `put_nd_ra_opts` action.
- `controller/pinctrl.c` periodic RA emission in the `pinctrl_handler` thread.
- `controller/lflow.c` translates the `put_nd_ra_opts` action to the OVS userspace handler.
- `northd/ovn-northd.8.xml` ¬ß"Ingress Table 8: ND_RA_OPTIONS" documentation.
- `ovn-nb.ovsschema` `Logical_Router_Port` column `options:ipv6_ra_configs` map.
- Pre-existing reference: Part 13.16 line 224, Part 18.0 ¬ßIPv6 ND.

**Real-world incident.** Q4 2024, an OpenStack production cluster with OVN 22.03. The customer enabled IPv6 SLAAC on 50 tenant LSes. The intermittent report: "IPv6 VMs lose connectivity after 30 minutes of activity". Investigation: tcpdump on hypervisor chassis-1 showed RAs emitted every 200s, but chassis-2 in the same cluster (hosting other VMs) showed no RA emit. `ovn-sbctl show` confirmed the LR was distributed across both chassis. Root cause: the `pinctrl` thread in ovn-controller on chassis-2 had crashed after an OOM event and did not restart the periodic RA timer. VMs on chassis-2 stopped receiving RA refreshes, the lifetime of 1800s expired, and they fell back to link-local. Fix: `systemctl restart ovn-controller` on chassis-2, plus a new monitoring alert for the `pinctrl_thread_alive` metric. Cross-link Part 13.7 ¬ßpinctrl thread.

**Synthetic exercise.**

```bash
sudo ovn-nbctl lr-add lr-ipv6
sudo ovn-nbctl lrp-add lr-ipv6 lrp-int 02:ac:10:00:00:01 2001:db8:1::1/64
sudo ovn-nbctl lrp-set-options lrp-int \
    ipv6_ra_configs:address_mode=slaac \
    ipv6_ra_configs:prefix=2001:db8:1::/64 \
    ipv6_ra_configs:max_interval=60 \
    ipv6_ra_configs:mtu=1450
sudo ovn-nbctl ls-add ls-ipv6
sudo ovn-nbctl lrp-add lr-ipv6 lrp-pub 02:ac:10:00:01:01 2001:db8:0::1/64
# Lab task 1: ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr-ipv6 | grep lr_in_nd_ra_options should show a priority 80 flow.
# Lab task 2: spawn an IPv6 VM on ls-ipv6 and verify that ip -6 addr shows an auto-configured 2001:db8:1::.../64 address.
# Lab task 3: tcpdump -ni vnet-vm 'icmp6 and ip6[40]==134' to count RAs every 60s.
# Lab task 4: remove the ipv6_ra_configs option and verify that the flow disappears and the VM stops receiving new RAs.
# Lab task 5: simulate an RS from the VM (`rdisc6 eth0`) and verify a solicited RA reply within 1s.
```

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.**
- Mode A "VM does not receive periodic RAs": the pinctrl thread crashed. Restart ovn-controller and check the `pinctrl_thread_alive` metric.
- Mode B "RA is missing the prefix": the `ipv6_ra_configs:prefix` option is unset. Set it explicitly.
- Mode C "VM receives the RA but does not configure": the option `address_mode=dhcpv6_stateful` requires an external DHCPv6 server. Change to `slaac` if no DHCPv6 is available.
- Mode D "wrong MTU in the RA": the `ipv6_ra_configs:mtu` option does not match the actual gateway MTU. Align it to 1450 (Geneve overhead).

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux: the traditional `radvd` daemon answers RSs and emits periodic RAs. OVN replaces it to achieve a distributed and declarative design. NSX-T DLR has integrated IPv6 SLAAC support that is similar but does not expose the pipeline. Cisco IOS: `ipv6 nd ra-interval` per interface. AWS VPC IPv6: implicit RA for IPv6 subnets, with no exposed timer.

---

## ¬ß13.19.20 LR_OUT_SNAT, forward SNAT for egress traffic

**Concept.** `LR_OUT_SNAT` is the Logical_Router egress logical stage that **applies SNAT** to outbound packets: it rewrites the source IP from the internal IP to the external IP configured in a NAT rule (`type==snat` or `type==dnat_and_snat`). Symmetric with `LR_IN_UNSNAT` in the opposite direction; together the two stages form the masquerade or floating IP pattern. Type: **stateful NAT applier** at the medium tier, subtype "egress forward SNAT".

**History and background.** The stage has existed since early OVN versions (OVN 2.6, 2017), when Numan Siddique first implemented NAT support. OVN 21.03 added the distributed gateway port, allowing SNAT to run distributed per-chassis rather than centralised. OVN 22.03 stabilised both modes (centralised and distributed). OVN 24.03 optimised compile by splitting `snat` and `dnat_and_snat` rules into two priority classes.

**Position in the architecture.** Logical stage 3 maps to OF table 43 (the LR egress pipeline has a different offset from ingress, per Part 13.16 line 253). It follows `LR_OUT_UNDNAT` (logical 1) and `LR_OUT_POST_UNDNAT` (logical 2) and precedes `LR_OUT_DELIVERY` (logical 4). Scope: per-packet, per-LR datapath, only egress packets whose source matches the logical_ip range of a SNAT rule.

**Role.** Main role: **rewrite the source IP from internal (logical_ip) to external (external_ip)** for packets going out to the Internet. Secondary role: commit a conntrack entry in the NAT zone so that the reply path `LR_IN_UNSNAT` un-NATs correctly. Scope: only stateful SNAT with new or established conntrack entries. Packets bypassing NAT (admin-disabled) fall through to priority 0 `next`.

**Why it exists.** Pre-existence pain: in a multi-tenant setup, an internal subnet (for example 10.0.0.0/24) is not routable on the Internet. For an internal VM to reach the Internet, the source must be translated to a routable external IP. Without a SNAT stage in the OVN pipeline, the operator must use iptables NAT on an external gateway chassis, which loses both "declarative NAT" (NBDB configuration) and distributed support. SNAT inside the OVN pipeline solves both: declarative via the `NAT` row, and able to run distributed.

**Problems it solves.** Specifically: (a) egress packets carry an internal source IP that is not routable, (b) the source must be rewritten to an external IP, (c) conntrack must commit so that the reply path knows the mapping. Solution: a priority 161 flow that matches `ip4.src == <logical_ip_range> && outport == "<gateway-port>"` and runs `ct(commit, zone=<nat_zone>, nat(src=<external_ip>:<port_range>));`. Scope: SNAT for egress only. The reply path is handled by UNSNAT on ingress. If the rule type is `dnat_and_snat` (floating IP), the priority is higher than 161 so it takes precedence over generic snat.

**Importance.** Medium. Required for every LR with an internal subnet that needs Internet or external network connectivity. If disabled, internal VMs are isolated from egress. Operator essential: understand that the pair LR_IN_UNSNAT and LR_OUT_SNAT is the core of masquerade and floating IP, sharing the same conntrack zone between the two stages.

**How it works.** The flow generator is `build_lrouter_out_snat_flow` (`northd/northd.c`, branch-22.03). The function iterates the NBDB `NAT` table; each entry with `type==snat` emits a priority 161 flow, and each `type==dnat_and_snat` emits a priority 162 flow. Template:

```
table=43 (lr_out_snat), priority=161,
  match=(ip4.src == 10.0.0.0/24 && outport == "lrp-pub"),
  action=(ct(commit, zone=8001, nat(src=192.0.2.10));)

table=43 (lr_out_snat), priority=162,
  match=(ip4.src == 10.0.0.5 && outport == "lrp-pub"),    /* floating IP */
  action=(ct(commit, zone=8001, nat(src=192.0.2.20));)

table=43 (lr_out_snat), priority=0,
  match=(1), action=(next;)
```

The action `ct(commit, nat(src=...))` parses in `lib/actions.c` to `ofpact_conntrack` with the NAT_SRC flag; the controller translates it to OVS `ct(commit, zone=NXM_NX_REG11[0..15], nat(src=192.0.2.10))`. The kernel module `nf_nat_module` rewrites the source IP field and the checksum. Invariant: after this stage, the source IP has been rewritten, the conntrack entry is committed, and the packet proceeds to `LR_OUT_DELIVERY` to output through a tunnel or an external patch port.

**How an operator masters this.** (1) **Read**: understand that priority 161 is generic SNAT and 162 is floating IP (dnat_and_snat). (2) **Modify**: `ovn-nbctl lr-nat-add lr-pub snat 192.0.2.10 10.0.0.0/24` for masquerade, or `ovn-nbctl lr-nat-add lr-pub dnat_and_snat 192.0.2.20 10.0.0.5` for floating IP. (3) **Observe**: `dpctl/dump-conntrack zone=<nat_zone>` shows entries with rewritten source. (4) **Debug**: if a VM cannot egress, use `ofproto/trace 'in_port=N,ip,ip_src=10.0.0.5,...'` to verify whether stage 43 rewrites the source.

**Classification.** Stage type: **stateful NAT applier** (subtype "egress forward SNAT"). Different from `LR_IN_UNSNAT` (reverse direction, ingress logical stage 4), and from `LS_IN_NAT_HAIRPIN` (hairpin SNAT on the LS, not the LR). Same NAT family as `LR_IN_DNAT` and `LR_OUT_UNDNAT`.

**Usage workflow.** When configuring masquerade SNAT for a subnet:
1. Create the LR and gateway port: `ovn-nbctl lr-add lr-pub && ovn-nbctl lrp-add lr-pub lrp-pub 02:ac:10:ff:00:01 192.0.2.1/24`.
2. Set the gateway chassis: `ovn-nbctl lrp-set-gateway-chassis lrp-pub chassis-1 priority=10`.
3. Add the SNAT rule: `ovn-nbctl lr-nat-add lr-pub snat 192.0.2.10 10.0.0.0/24`.
4. Verify the LR_OUT_SNAT flow: `ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr-pub | grep lr_out_snat` should show flows at priorities 161 and 0.
5. Test: from VM `10.0.0.5`, curl the Internet. tcpdump on the uplink should show src=`192.0.2.10`, and `dpctl/dump-conntrack zone <nat_zone>` should have the mapping entry.
6. Floating IP: `ovn-nbctl lr-nat-add lr-pub dnat_and_snat 192.0.2.20 10.0.0.5` adds a priority 162 flow for the specific VM.

**When something goes wrong.** Typical symptoms: (a) "the internal VM cannot reach the Internet", which suggests a missing SNAT rule or a downed gateway chassis. (b) "egress packets still show src=10.0.0.5 instead of 192.0.2.10", which suggests the SNAT rule does not match the logical_ip range. Diagnostic:
- Step 1: `ovn-nbctl lr-nat-list lr-pub` to confirm the rule exists.
- Step 2: `ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr-pub | grep lr_out_snat` to confirm the flow is generated.
- Step 3: `ofproto/trace 'in_port=lrp-int,ip,ip_src=10.0.0.5,ip_dst=8.8.8.8'` to simulate egress.
- Step 4: if the trace stops at stage 43 without rewriting, check the match expression (does the logical_ip overlap with the VM source).
- Step 5: `dpctl/dump-conntrack zone=<nat_zone>` to see new entries when traffic flows.

Cross-link Part 13.3 ¬ßNAT, Part 19.0 ¬ßSNAT troubleshoot.

**Tightly related to.** Tightly coupled: the NBDB `NAT` table (columns `type`, `external_ip`, `logical_ip`, `external_mac`, `logical_port`), `LR_IN_UNSNAT` (the corresponding reverse path), `LR_OUT_UNDNAT` (the ingress UNSNAT counterpart, at the start of the egress pipeline), the NAT conntrack zone (shared with UNSNAT), `LR_OUT_DELIVERY` (the downstream consumer), and the kernel `nf_nat_module`. Loose: `Logical_Router_Port.options:gateway_mtu` (LARGER_PKTS interaction when the SNAT-translated packet exceeds the MTU).

**Version differences.**
- OVN 2.6 (2017): introduces the stage along with SNAT/DNAT support.
- OVN 20.06: adds the `dnat_and_snat` type for the floating IP pattern.
- OVN 21.03: distributed gateway port lets SNAT run distributed (each chassis has a local rule).
- OVN 22.03 (baseline): the NAT zone is clearly separated from the routing zone.
- OVN 23.06: adds the `allowed_ext_ips` column on `NAT` to restrict the egress IP pool.
- OVN 24.03: officially splits the priority classes for `snat` (161) and `dnat_and_snat` (162) in the template.

**C√°ch quan s√°t + x√°c minh.**

```
$ ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr-pub | grep -A2 "lr_out_snat"
  table=3 (lr_out_snat), priority=161,
    match=(ip4.src == 10.0.0.0/24 && outport == "lrp-pub"),
    action=(ct(commit, zone=8001, nat(src=192.0.2.10));)
  table=3 (lr_out_snat), priority=0,
    match=(1), action=(next;)

$ ovs-ofctl dump-flows br-int table=43 | head -3
  cookie=0x4, table=43, n_packets=512, ip,nw_src=10.0.0.0/24,
  metadata=0x4, actions=ct(commit,zone=NXM_NX_REG11[0..15],nat(src=192.0.2.10))

$ ovs-appctl dpctl/dump-conntrack zone=8001 | head -3
  tcp,orig=(src=10.0.0.5,dst=8.8.8.8,sport=4001,dport=443),
       reply=(src=8.8.8.8,dst=192.0.2.10,sport=443,dport=4001),
       protoinfo=(state=ESTABLISHED), zone=8001
```

**Source code reference.**
- `northd/northd.c` `build_lrouter_out_snat_flow()`: the function that emits flows for this stage (verified at branch-22.03).
- `lib/actions.c` parser for the `ct(commit, nat(src=...))` action with the NAT_SRC flag.
- `controller/lflow.c` translates `ct(nat)` to OVS with zone REG11.
- `northd/ovn-northd.8.xml` ¬ß"Egress Table 3: SNAT" documentation.
- `ovn-nb.ovsschema` table `NAT` (columns `type`, `external_ip`, `logical_ip`, `external_mac`, `logical_port`).
- Kernel: `net/netfilter/nf_nat_core.c` `nf_nat_setup_info()` forward mapping.
- Pre-existing reference: Part 13.16 line 253, Part 13.3 ¬ßNAT type catalog.

**Real-world incident.** Q1 2025, an OpenStack production cluster with OVN 22.03. The customer migrated from the Neutron L3 agent to the OVN distributed gateway. After migration, some tenant VMs could not reach the Internet, while other customers worked normally. Investigation: `ovn-nbctl lr-nat-list lr-tenant-A` showed the SNAT rule was complete. `ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr-tenant-A | grep lr_out_snat` showed the priority 161 flow generated correctly. `ofproto/trace` on the hypervisor hosting tenant A VMs: the trace stopped at stage 43 with `outport != "lrp-pub"`, meaning the match expression failed. Root cause: after migrating, the customer created a gateway LRP named `lrp-public` (a typo with an extra hyphen breaking the naming convention), while the NAT rule referenced logical_port `lrp-pub`. ovn-northd compiled successfully but generated a flow with the wrong match outport. Fix: rename the LRP back to the `lrp-pub` convention and recreate the NAT rule with the correct `logical_port`. The drop rate fell to 0 percent. Cross-link Part 13.3 ¬ßNAT.logical_port column.

**Synthetic exercise.**

```bash
sudo ovn-nbctl lr-add lr-snat
sudo ovn-nbctl lrp-add lr-snat lrp-pub 02:ac:10:ff:00:01 192.0.2.1/24
sudo ovn-nbctl lrp-set-gateway-chassis lrp-pub chassis-1 priority=10
sudo ovn-nbctl ls-add ls-int
sudo ovn-nbctl lrp-add lr-snat lrp-int 02:ac:10:00:00:01 10.0.0.1/24
# Generic SNAT (masquerade)
sudo ovn-nbctl lr-nat-add lr-snat snat 192.0.2.10 10.0.0.0/24
# Floating IP
sudo ovn-nbctl lr-nat-add lr-snat dnat_and_snat 192.0.2.20 10.0.0.5
# Lab task 1: ovn-sbctl lflow-list lr-snat | grep lr_out_snat should show flows at priorities 161, 162, and 0.
# Lab task 2: from VM 10.0.0.10, curl the Internet. tcpdump on the uplink should show src=192.0.2.10 (snat).
# Lab task 3: from VM 10.0.0.5, curl the Internet. tcpdump should show src=192.0.2.20 (floating IP, priority 162 wins).
# Lab task 4: ofproto/trace 'in_port=lrp-int,ip,ip_src=10.0.0.10,ip_dst=8.8.8.8' to verify stage 43 rewrites.
# Lab task 5: delete the snat rule and verify that VM 10.0.0.10 cannot egress while VM 10.0.0.5 still egresses through the floating IP.
```

**Common failures and diagnostic signals.**
- Mode A "egress packet source is not rewritten": the logical_ip range does not match the VM source. Check the CIDR is correct.
- Mode B "floating IP does not win priority": rule order conflict. Priority 162 is for `dnat_and_snat`. Recreate the rule with the correct type.
- Mode C "reply is not un-SNAT-ed": NAT zone mismatch between LR_OUT_SNAT (commit) and LR_IN_UNSNAT (reverse). Check the ovn-northd zone allocation.
- Mode D "conntrack table exhaustion": many VMs share one SNAT external IP and exhaust the port range. Raise `nf_conntrack_max` or add multiple external IPs to the SNAT pool.

**Comparison with other systems.** Linux iptables: `iptables -t nat -A POSTROUTING -s 10.0.0.0/24 -j SNAT --to-source 192.0.2.10` is a one-to-one analog. OVN declares the rule through a NAT row instead of imperatively through iptables. Cisco ASA: `nat (inside,outside) source dynamic any interface` is the masquerade equivalent. NSX-T DLR: per-uplink distributed SNAT rules. AWS NAT Gateway: managed SNAT that does not expose the pipeline.

---

## ¬ß13.19.21 LS_IN_ARP_ND_RSP ‚ÄĒ bŠĽô trŠļ£ lŠĽĚi ARP/ND nŠĽôi bŠĽô trong pipeline LS

**Kh√°i niŠĽám.** `LS_IN_ARP_ND_RSP` l√† stage logic ingress cŠĽßa Logical_Switch chuy√™n **chŠļ∑n v√† trŠļ£ lŠĽĚi** ARP request (IPv4) c√Ļng IPv6 Neighbor Solicitation (NS) ngay tŠļ°i chassis nguŠĽďn, kh√īng cho broadcast/multicast lan ra fabric. ovn-northd compile danh s√°ch MAC + IP ńĎ√£ biŠļŅt cŠĽßa mŠĽći LSP trong c√Ļng LS th√†nh Logical_Flow priority cao tr√™n stage n√†y; khi packet ARP request match, stage tŠĽĪ build ARP reply (hoŠļ∑c Neighbor Advertisement vŠĽõi IPv6) bŠļĪng action `eth.dst = eth.src; eth.src = <MAC>; arp.op = 2; arp.tha = arp.sha; arp.sha = <MAC>; arp.tpa = arp.spa; arp.spa = <IP>; outport = inport; flags.loopback = 1; output;`. LoŠļ°i: **pipeline ARP/ND short-circuit responder**, sub-loŠļ°i "known-IP local responder".

**LŠĽčch sŠĽ≠ + bŠĽĎi cŠļ£nh.** Stage c√≥ mŠļ∑t tŠĽę OVN 2.5 (2016) c√Ļng vŠĽõi m√ī h√¨nh distributed L2 m√† Justin Pettit + Russell Bryant ńĎŠļ©y v√†o. Tr∆įŠĽõc ńĎ√≥, OVN s∆° khai (2.3-2.4) phŠļ£i flood ARP request qua tunnel mesh ńĎŠļŅn mŠĽći chassis ńĎŠĽÉ c√≥ chassis target reply, g√Ęy "ARP storm" trong cluster lŠĽõn (1000+ port LSP). OVN 2.5 ńĎ∆įa ra `arp` table cho LS, OVN 2.7 n√Ęng cŠļ•p t√™n th√†nh `LS_IN_ARP_ND_RSP` thŠĽĎng nhŠļ•t vŠĽõi IPv6 ND, OVN 22.06 th√™m stage trung gian `LS_IN_PRE_ARP_ND_RSP` ńĎŠĽÉ xŠĽ≠ l√Ĺ hint tr∆įŠĽõc. Stage tŠļ≠n dŠĽ•ng kiŠļŅn thŠĽ©c ovn-northd c√≥ sŠļĶn vŠĽĀ mŠĽći MAC/IP trong c√Ļng LS ńĎŠĽÉ tr√°nh broadcast.

**VŠĽč tr√≠ trong kiŠļŅn tr√ļc.** Stage logic 18 trong LS_IN pipeline, √°nh xŠļ° OF table 26 tr√™n br-int (theo 13.16 d√≤ng 169). ńźŠĽ©ng sau `LS_IN_DNS_LOOKUP/RESPONSE` (stage 16-17) v√† tr∆įŠĽõc `LS_IN_DHCP_OPTIONS/RESPONSE` (stage 19-20) c√Ļng `LS_IN_L2_LKUP` (stage 24). Scope: per-packet (chŠĽČ ARP/ND), per-LS datapath, per-LSP (mŠĽói LSP biŠļŅt addresses cŠĽßa m√¨nh + thŠļ•y MAC/IP cŠĽßa LSP kh√°c c√Ļng LS). Stage chŠļ°y tr√™n chassis nguŠĽďn (chassis cŠĽßa VM gŠĽ≠i ARP), kh√īng broadcast cross-chassis.

**Vai tr√≤.** Vai tr√≤ ch√≠nh: **giŠļ£m ARP/ND broadcast traffic** tŠĽę O(N√óM) (N LSP √ó M chassis) xuŠĽĎng O(1) (local reply tŠļ°i chassis nguŠĽďn). Vai tr√≤ phŠĽ•: cung cŠļ•p ARP suppression mŠļ∑c ńĎŠĽčnh cho mŠĽći LS kh√īng cŠļßn config th√™m; ńĎŠļ£m bŠļ£o VM nhŠļ≠n ARP reply ngay cŠļ£ khi LSP target nŠļĪm tr√™n chassis kh√°c m√† tunnel ch∆įa up. PhŠļ°m vi vai tr√≤: chŠĽČ trŠļ£ lŠĽĚi cho IP ńĎ√£ ńĎ∆įŠĽ£c declare trong `Logical_Switch_Port.addresses` (known IPs); IP "unknown" (LSP c√≥ `addresses=unknown` hoŠļ∑c `dynamic` ch∆įa cŠļ•p) vŠļęn flood qua MC_FLOOD.

**V√¨ sao sinh ra.** Pre-existence pain: trong DC Šļ£o ho√° lŠĽõn (1000-10000 VM), ARP request l√† loŠļ°i broadcast traffic d√†y ńĎŠļ∑c nhŠļ•t (mŠĽói connection mŠĽõi phŠļ£i resolve MAC). NŠļŅu OVS flood ARP qua tunnel mesh, mŠĽói ARP request tŠĽę VM-A tŠļ°o N√ó(M-1) packet tr√™n fabric ‚Üí bandwidth + CPU consumption tuyŠļŅn t√≠nh theo cluster size. Solution external (Linux bridge l2population, EVPN Type-2) tŠĽďn tŠļ°i nh∆įng phŠĽ• thuŠĽôc control plane ri√™ng. OVN tŠļ≠n dŠĽ•ng NBDB ńĎ√£ c√≥ sŠļĶn full address knowledge ‚Üí tŠĽĪ sinh local responder per-chassis ‚Üí loŠļ°i bŠĽŹ broadcast ho√†n to√†n cho known IP.

**VŠļ•n ńĎŠĽĀ giŠļ£i quyŠļŅt.** CŠĽ• thŠĽÉ: (a) ARP request cho `tpa = X` (target IP) cŠļßn resolve th√†nh MAC, (b) X c√≥ thŠĽÉ nŠļĪm tr√™n chassis kh√°c, (c) flood to√†n cluster g√Ęy overhead. C√°ch giŠļ£i: priority 50 flow `match=(arp.op == 1 && arp.tpa == <known_ip>)` action build reply bŠļĪng `eth.dst = eth.src; ...; outport = inport; flags.loopback = 1; output;`. Packet quay ng∆įŠĽ£c vŠĽĀ sender ngay tŠļ°i stage 18, kh√īng chŠļ°m ńĎŠļŅn L2_LKUP ńĎŠĽÉ flood. IPv6 t∆į∆°ng tŠĽĪ vŠĽõi `match=(nd_ns && nd.target == <known_ipv6>)` action build NA. PhŠļ°m vi giŠļ£i quyŠļŅt: chŠĽČ known IP; unknown IP fall xuŠĽĎng priority 0 next ‚Üí eventually flood qua L2_LKUP MC_FLOOD.

**TŠļßm quan trŠĽćng.** MŠĽ©c trung b√¨nh quan trŠĽćng. BŠļĮt buŠĽôc cho mŠĽći triŠĽÉn khai OVN production (mŠļ∑c ńĎŠĽčnh bŠļ≠t, kh√īng cŠļßn config). NŠļŅu disable bŠļĪng `ovn-nbctl set Logical_Switch ls1 other_config:vlan-passthru=true` hoŠļ∑c per-LSP `disable_arp_nd_rsp=true` th√¨ ARP storm trŠĽü lŠļ°i ngay khi cluster v∆įŠĽ£t 200 VM. Engineer must-know: hiŠĽÉu 5 gate ńĎiŠĽĀu kiŠĽán northd kiŠĽÉm tra tr∆įŠĽõc khi sinh flow (chi tiŠļŅt ŠĽü PhŠļßn 18.0); biŠļŅt suppression kh√īng hoŠļ°t ńĎŠĽông cho LSP `addresses=unknown` hoŠļ∑c type `external`.

**C∆° chŠļŅ hoŠļ°t ńĎŠĽông.** H√†m sinh flow: `build_lswitch_arp_nd_responder_known_ips()` trong `northd/northd.c` (verified branch-22.03). H√†m duyŠĽát mŠĽći LSP trong LS, vŠĽõi mŠĽói (MAC, IP) pair trong `port_security` hoŠļ∑c `addresses`, sinh 1 flow priority 50:

```
table=26 (ls_in_arp_rsp), priority=50,
  match=(arp.tpa == 10.0.0.5 && arp.op == 1),
  action=(eth.dst = eth.src;
          eth.src = aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01;
          arp.op = 2; /* ARP reply */
          arp.tha = arp.sha;
          arp.sha = aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01;
          arp.tpa = arp.spa;
          arp.spa = 10.0.0.5;
          outport = inport;
          flags.loopback = 1;
          output;)

table=26 (ls_in_arp_rsp), priority=50,
  match=(nd_ns && ip6.dst == {ff02::1:ff00:5/104, fe80::aabb...} && nd.target == fe80::aabb...),
  action=(nd_na { eth.src = aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01;
                  ip6.src = fe80::aabb...;
                  nd.target = fe80::aabb...;
                  nd.tll = aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01;
                  outport = inport;
                  flags.loopback = 1;
                  output; };)

table=26 (ls_in_arp_rsp), priority=0,
  match=(1), action=(next;)
```

Action `nd_na` l√† OVN extended action (kh√īng phŠļ£i OF chuŠļ©n) ‚ÄĒ controller compile xuŠĽĎng sequence OVS action thao t√°c trŠĽĪc tiŠļŅp ICMPv6 ND payload trong kernel datapath. `flags.loopback = 1` cŠļßn thiŠļŅt v√¨ packet ra c√Ļng `inport`; bŠĽŹ flag n√†y th√¨ OVS sŠļĹ drop packet do anti-loopback default. Invariant: sau stage n√†y, ARP request cho known IP ‚Üí ARP reply quay ng∆įŠĽ£c; ARP request cho unknown IP ‚Üí next ‚Üí eventually flood qua MC_FLOOD ŠĽü L2_LKUP.

**C√°ch kŠĽĻ s∆į vŠļ≠n h√†nh th√†nh thŠļ°o.** (1) **ńźŠĽćc**: hiŠĽÉu priority 50 flow l√† responder cho known IP, priority 0 l√† fallback cho unknown. (2) **SŠĽ≠a**: bŠļ≠t/tŠļĮt qua `ovn-nbctl lsp-set-options lsp1 disable_arp_nd_rsp=true|false`; th√™m IP qua `ovn-nbctl lsp-set-addresses lsp1 "<MAC> <IP1> <IP2>"`. (3) **Quan s√°t**: `ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls1 | grep ls_in_arp_rsp` ńĎŠļŅm sŠĽĎ flow priority 50. (4) **Debug**: nŠļŅu VM-A gŠĽ≠i ARP cho VM-B (c√Ļng LS) m√† nhŠļ≠n flood thay v√¨ local reply, d√Ļng `ovn-trace ls1 'inport=="lsp-A" && eth.src==<MAC-A> && arp.op==1 && arp.tpa==<IP-B>'` xem c√≥ hit priority 50 ŠĽü stage 18 hay r∆°i xuŠĽĎng priority 0.

**Ph√Ęn loŠļ°i.** LoŠļ°i stage: **ARP/ND short-circuit responder** (subtype "known-IP local replier"). Kh√°c `LR_IN_IP_INPUT` (router self ARP responder cho IP cŠĽßa LRP), kh√°c `LS_IN_DHCP_OPTIONS` (DHCP server inline cŇ©ng c√Ļng pattern "intercept + reply" nh∆įng cho DHCP UDP). C√Ļng hŠĽć "pipeline-inline responder" vŠĽõi `LR_IN_ND_RA_OPTIONS`.

**Quy tr√¨nh sŠĽ≠ dŠĽ•ng.**
1. TŠļ°o LS + LSP vŠĽõi address known: `ovn-nbctl ls-add ls1 && ovn-nbctl lsp-add ls1 lsp-vm1 && ovn-nbctl lsp-set-addresses lsp-vm1 "aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01 10.0.0.5"`
2. Verify flow sinh: `ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls1 | grep "arp.tpa == 10.0.0.5"` thŠļ•y priority 50
3. Verify tŠĽę VM kh√°c c√Ļng LS: `arping 10.0.0.5` tŠĽę VM-B ‚Üí nhŠļ≠n reply MAC `aa:bb:cc:dd:ee:01`
4. Test suppression: `tcpdump -i geneve_sys_6081 arp` tr√™n chassis target ‚Üí KH√ĒNG thŠļ•y ARP request tŠĽę VM-B (v√¨ local reply tŠļ°i chassis nguŠĽďn)
5. Test fallback: tŠļ°o LSP `lsp-vnf` vŠĽõi `addresses=unknown` ‚Üí ARP cho IP n√†o ńĎ√≥ match LSP n√†y phŠļ£i flood (priority 0 next ‚Üí MC_FLOOD)

**Khi xŠļ£y ra sŠĽĪ cŠĽĎ.** Symptom ńĎiŠĽÉn h√¨nh: (a) "VM-A kh√īng resolve ńĎ∆įŠĽ£c MAC cŠĽßa VM-B mŠļ∑c d√Ļ c√Ļng LS" ‚Üí suspect priority 50 flow kh√īng sinh hoŠļ∑c bŠĽč disable. (b) "ARP storm b√Ļng ph√°t khi cluster scale l√™n 500 VM" ‚Üí suspect responder bŠĽč tŠļĮt hoŠļ∑c nhiŠĽĀu LSP `addresses=unknown`. Diagnostic:
- Step 1: `ovn-nbctl lsp-get-addresses lsp-vm-B` xem IP ńĎ√£ declare ńĎ√ļng ch∆įa
- Step 2: `ovn-sbctl lflow-list ls1 | grep "arp.tpa == <IP-B>"` xem flow priority 50 tŠĽďn tŠļ°i ch∆įa
- Step 3: `ovn-trace ls1 'inport=="lsp-A" && arp.tpa==<IP-B>'` xem stage 18 c√≥ match priority 50 kh√īng
